<a id="t-c80018e86c"></a>
## Thread: Is OO COBOL relevant today or is it unnecessary? — 57 msgs

_Topics: [`Object-oriented COBOL`](topics.md#oo)_

### Is OO COBOL relevant today or is it unnecessary?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-11-09T14:27:08+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8jrmbeFvo6U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
As an OO COBOL user for nearly 20 years now, and seeing some fairly direct 
criticism in the "Phase 1 - Rosetta bottles of beer" thread, I thought it 
might be interesting to see how people feel about OO COBOL, and why they 
feel that way (particularly if they have never used it, although not having 
used something doesn't preclude people having an opinion about it :-))

Jimmy Gavan posted an OO COBOL example of the well-known "Bottles of beer" 
problem.

To an "ininitiated" (read "unfamiliar with OO COBOL...") eye this code looks 
like a severe departure from Standard procedural COBOL. It provokes a strong 
reaction from some people.

I don't intend to criticise Jimmy's code in any way, here, but the following 
factors should be kept in mind by people used to standard COBOL, when they 
review this code.

1. It is written using Micro Focus language extensions which will be 
unfamiliar to many people.
2. It is just one interpretation of an OO COBOL solution to the problem.
3. It was not intended to be a textbook example of "how to write OO COBOL"
4. It DOES demonstrate at least one useful facility of OO COBOL (recursion).

History has shown that ANYONE posting code here will be subject to criticism 
(not necessarily a bad thing... as long as you are not a sensitive flower or 
easily discouraged) and it is the nature of programming that programmers 
will differ in the way they do things.

However, on this occasion some of the objections don't seem to be about the 
code, but rather the whole concept of Object Oriented Programming in COBOL.

As someone who has walked that path I'd like to offer the following:

1.Most people who write OO COBOL are NOT doing so because they want to 
emulate what is available in other OO languages.
2. Some of us who seized OO COBOL when it first became readily available 
(and Jimmy and myself are 2 survivors from that camp) saw it as bringing the 
benefits of Object Orientation without having to pick up another language. 
(For myself, I can only say I was mistaken about that; I never really 
grasped OO COBOL until I taught myself Java.)
3. Depending on what you want to do, OO COBOL can be an excellent way to do 
it.

I used OO COBOL to write components for many years. I'd probably still be 
doing it if events hadn't transpired to make me think again about using 
COBOL as a development language, at all.

Those events DID transpire and led to me moving away from COBOL. So, at 
least for me, the question posed by the topic should have the "OO" removed 
from it.

For batch processing there is no doubt that COBOL is an excellent choice, 
now, and for at least the immediate future. (The question THAT raises is 
whether batch processing is an approach that will proceed far into the 
future or whether it will be subsumed by online systems and data 
warehousing, running on more powerful and distributed processors.)

For network processing, the future is in Objects and Layers. SOME kind of OO 
processing needs to be adoted to move into this camp.

The success of languages like Java, C++, C# etc. is NOT because they are 
good for batch processing...

Howard posted an excellent response in the thread referenced above, where he 
pointed out that OO COBOL can be used to leverage existing COBOL into the 
world of Objects and Layers.

He is absolutely right. Using OO COBOL to wrap existing procedural code can 
mean that much of a company's existing critical code base can be brought 
into the world of Objects and doesn't need to be re-developed. (At least, 
not right away. Using OO COBOL can buy you time.)

If nothing else, at least it provides a strategic option; you MAY choose to 
re-develop in a new llanguage or using a new language, but you don't HAVE 
to.

I have always advocated not throwing the baby out with the bath water, and 
spent several years developing tools to assist migration from Procedural 
COBOL into the world of Objects and Layers using OO COBOL, so that COBOL 
Objects can interact with Objects in other languages as full citizens in the 
Brave New World of the Network.

So, the first part of my personal opinion on the topic would be: "Yes, OO 
COBOL is not only relevant, but very useful, for extending the life of  an 
existing COBOL code base."

Wrapping existing code with the dozen or so lines of OO COBOL needed to 
create Classes in COBOL provides advantages far beyond the approach of 
simply re-compiling for .Net.  (Succesful migration is not just about 
platforms, it is about architecture...)

The second part of my response is more problematic.

It has to do with code, and tools, and development.

We've seen the reponses to the OO code Jimmy posted.

It DOES look "strange" and "non-COBOL like" when you first encounter it.

And there is a plethora of jargon surrounding OOP, just as there is in other 
aspects of technology. But it is NOT "unknowable" or "mystic" in a 
scriptural sense.

OO COBOL (although it varies slightly in different implementations; I've 
always thought the Micro Focus implementation is a bit "closer to the metal" 
than the Fujitsu one, for example, but that is just a personal opinion and 
arguable...) DOES allow you to implement the key concepts (like Classes, 
Inheritance, Interfacing, Polymorphism, etc.) of an Object Oriented 
Language.

The question this raises is why would you WANT to? (That one is answered 
above) Given that you WANT to, why use OO COBOL when there are other OO 
languages which were designed for that purpose?

This is a much stickier wicket.

I guess the immediate response would be: "Because I can get the OO leverage 
I want for the Network, without having to learn a new language."

This is probably true for some people; it wasn't for me. That particular 
promise was not fulfilled by OO COBOL.

These days I use a language designed for OO (C#) and find it much more 
powerful than COBOL ever was, so I could say: "No, for OO development, OO 
COBOL is NOT relevant today. There are much better tools available for this 
job."

BOTTOM LINE:

As you have seen from the discussion above, I find myself in a "yes"/"no" 
situation and the answer is not totally clear.

If OO COBOL disappeared tomorrow, would I miss it?  Yes, definitely. But not 
for new development.

Do I still build components in OO COBOL? No. I still use component based 
design and development, but not in COBOL.

Am I sorry I moved away from COBOL as a development language? No. I wish I 
had grasped the nettle and done it sooner.

This post has been made in order to explore some of the implications of 
using OO COBOL a bit more than superficially, and to hopefully show that 
using it is not about trying to write C++ in COBOL.

I'd be interested to hear what other people think.

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Is OO COBOL relevant today or is it unnecessary?

- **From:** Paul <paul-nospamatall.raulerson@mac.com>
- **Date:** 2010-11-08T22:58:25-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4cd8d4f1$0$14786$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`
- **References:** `<8jrmbeFvo6U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Hey Pete -
I like your thinking, and decided to go ahead and take a few minutes 
and write up a response to it. This is, of course, my opinion, not 
stone cold facts.
First, Objects. Objects are great. For Design and depending upon the 
language and implementation, not too bad for implementation. But when 
you take them to extremes, you wind up in some “interesting” situations.
SmallTalk is probably the most object-oriented language around. You can 
design correct SmallTalk programs with object layer and object layer, 
and get reliable behavior from them. Methods and data are wonderfully 
encapsulated, and you just don’t have problems with buffer overflows 
and such.
All the IBM “VisualAge” products were based upon a SmallTalk front end, 
from VisualAge COBOL to VisualAge Generator. (There was one exception I 
know of - VisualAge RPG.) You could design some nice GUI’s with no real 
programming, and events were encapsulated in methods that were easy to 
code with program snippets.
But- when you get down to the non-visual program logic, essentially, 
read a record, do something with it, and write it back out, or doing 
intense calculations, SmallTalk became a serious nuisance. You can not  
just read a record for instance. You wind up asking a method to 
populate a collection, or some other abstraction that really is more of 
a interference than a help.
So what all that means to me is that Objects are great for design - but 
like normalized databases, must usually be “denormalized” 
(de-objectified?) to actually do the work efficiently. I think most 
people agree with that point of view, at least people who have done 
large projects (especially real time projects) tend to feel that way. 
It really works, and you find that U.S. Government Defense projects are 
almost always specified to use something along these lines these days. 
For example, the High Level Design Document might be specified to be 
compilable.
Languages like Ada really show this mixture very very well. Highly 
Object oriented, both in design and implementation at the top level 
design and implementation. Very procedural at the low levels when you 
need it to be. In a controlled and usable manner.
Of course, at this point, most people ask what the heck these “Objects” 
are anyway… as they appear to be mysterious things out there that 
refuse to reveal their innards to a normal person’s inspection.
Let me work up to this a bit - in the bad old days - say the 1950’s - 
programming was a wild and wooly adventure. Assembler Languages were 
all the rage, and for good reason - compilers usually had to load 
stages from tape. Assemblers tended to fit into memory!   A typical 
COBOL compile job could take a long time to run - hours even depending 
upon how many passes were needed.
Programmers learned to do things like use registers well and write 
inline code with well defined branch operations. Remember testing the 
negative options first? It really helped on some machines.
But anyway, things like good memory management, how to optimize code, 
good branch management (how to use GOTO, B, BAL, BAS, JSCI, and other 
processor dependent techniques were learned, and more importantly 
documented. We also learned how to write codes in modules, and the 
basics of encapsulation. (JSR/JSCI instructions on some machines would 
save all the state information, jump to and execute a subroutine, 
return to the calling routine and restore the state. State usually 
being registers, but sometimes more.)
In the next stage of evolution, we learned how to do the same thing 
with high level languages like COBOL. This built of course, on the 
stuff the Assembler people had already learned. It was very common for 
instance, to have the COBOL compiler generate assembler code, which was 
then studied intensively to figure out the best ways to do things. Sure 
a lot of this was driven by hardware limitations of day, but the end 
result of this was “Structured Programming” - structured flow charts 
and so forth. In other words, we learned how to apply the good things 
we learned in the assembler days to high level code, and we learned how 
to design code in ways that helped ensure the  implementation of that 
design was accurate.
That of course, inevitably led to better programs, not only in terms of 
efficiency, but the programs were more correct. Hardware of course, 
progressed to the point where most development was online, and as 
hardware got better, the programs running on that hardware pushed more 
and more into new capabilities.
Minicomputers entered at around this stage, and things really took off. 
Vaxen, for example, while having good “batch” facilities, were 
primarily an online system, as were TOPS-10 “mainframes”, Wang VS, Data 
General, and a slew of others. Minicomputers took naturally to high 
level languages - mostly because of the cost. It wasn’t any more 
expensive to run an online program than it was to run a batch program, 
or at least, not much. Besides, online systems were fun.
So the greatest number of us “old farts” really got to use 
Minicomputers as our primary leaning platforms - the mainframes were 
just too darn expensive for people to learn on.
And then the MicroComputer hit, and everyone and their Aunts and Uncles 
were learning to program- usually in Basic. People got results. A whole 
generation or two of today’s IT experts grew up with Applesoft  Basic, 
or TRS80 Basic, or Coco Basic, or IBM Basic. Those basics require one 
to learn how to code subroutines and well mannered GOTO’s - if one 
wants to have a program that runs.
Of course, COBOL as quickly available on micro computers too - though 
it was so darn expensive that only large companies could afford it.
And while this was happening, another player snuck into the game - Unix 
was being used by more and more people every day - on centralized 
systems like Vaxen, or on Sun Workstations. Unix became the lingua 
franca of IT, and with Unix, one must inevitable learn C.
Arguably, C is a high level implementation of PDP-11 assembler - at 
least I think so and I now C and PDP-11 Macro assembly very well. :) 
The point being however, that all the good things we learned in 
assembler are pretty much available in C. And a LOT of the good things 
we learned in high level languages are also available in C. C lends 
itself well to structured design.  Just like COBOL.
During the late 1980s and early 1990s, we went through a dramatic stage 
of software thinking, during which time you could easily hear five 
different development and design philosophies just while eating lunch. 
Data Flow Diagrams emerged, along with UML, RAD, Top Down, Bottom Up, 
Layered Virtual Machines, and more.
Okay, I will get to the point - what we call objects today can trace 
their ancestry back to those subroutines in assembler, and paragraphs 
in COBOL, and functions in C, and data section records, and Pascal 
functions and subroutines, and so on.
In other words - they are, in general, the embodiment of just about 
every good practice and technique we IT people have learned over the 
past 60 years or so. Some implementations are better than other - C++ 
is awful in my opinion, while Ada is near perfect.

But that is really they are - fancy subroutines that embody good practices.

COBOL can make great use of methods and object oriented programming, 
but mostly when it is dealing with outside connections, GUIS, or when 
accessing some system services.
CURRENT-TIME for example, is in my opinion an example of a well used 
COBOL object.

I know, that is kind of a radical point of view, but it is what it is. :)

-Paul



On 2010-11-08 19:27:08 -0600, Pete Dashwood said:

> As an OO COBOL user for nearly 20 years now, and seeing some fairly 
> direct criticism in the "Phase 1 - Rosetta bottles of beer" thread, I 
…[147 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Is OO COBOL relevant today or is it unnecessary?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

Paul wrote:
> Hey Pete -
> I like your thinking, and decided to go ahead and take a few minutes
> and write up a response to it. This is, of course, my opinion, not
> stone cold facts.

No problem (as long asthe difference is clear... :-))

> First, Objects. Objects are great. For Design and depending upon the
> language and implementation, not too bad for implementation. But when
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> of a interference than a help.

I think we have established during past discussion here that OOP is NOT 
ideally suited to batch processing.

If we haven't, that would be my position.

Having said that, I just finished writing a tool for batch processing which 
is written in C# and is object oriented... Some of the problems I 
encountered (like the GUI freezing and appearing to hang) were caused 
precisely because OO is NOT good for batch processing. The same thread was 
running everything. (including servicing GUI events while running the 
batch). Once I implemented multi-threading, it was fine.

A batch script is usually a better solution (for batch processing) because 
it isn't driving a GUI front-end (or servicing requests from "somewhere 
else")

In this case there were other factors that necessitated it being point and 
click.

Generally, batch processing works better with a single code copy being 
serially reused, as Richard observed in his post below. Still, there are 
some occasions where it might be advantageous to have several objects, or 
instances of a Class, running simultaneously, even in a batch process... 
Updating a DB is one possible scenario. The DB may have several sub-DBs and 
you may want to update all of them independently (and simultaneously), as 
part of record processing in a batch process. Rather than doing it serially 
with a single code unit you could do it in parallel with multiple instances.

> So what all that means to me is that Objects are great for design -
> but like normalized databases, must usually be "denormalized"
> (de-objectified?) to actually do the work efficiently.

Based on a sample of SmallTalk?

I don't think that's fair... :-)

OOP has come a long way since SmallTalk. It isn't that objects need to be 
de-objectified; it is just a matter of what you want the Class (from which 
the object is derived) to do.

And, as we are generally agreeing, there are classes of processing like 
batch, for which OOP is not ideal. (It can do it,but there are probably 
better ways...)


> I think most
> people agree with that point of view, at least people who have done
> large projects (especially real time projects) tend to feel that way.

Not me. (And I have done some very large projects :-)) If you're going to 
use OOD then implement it with OOP. There's really no need to "de-objectify" 
your objects.

(Any more than there is usually a need to "de-normalize" your RDB. I've sat 
in with teams who were proposing to do that. Invariably it was for their own 
convenience, not for increased effficiency. I wouldn't allow this on any 
project unless the DBA OKed it and agreed it would improve actual 
performance.) I'm not saying I would NEVER do it, but I'd want a compelling 
case before permitting it. (So, far, I have seen some candidate cases, but 
tnone that really required it...)

If you do proper modelling during design and cover the use case scenarios 
completely, you should be confident that your objects are good to go. (If 
they work properly on paper during a use case walkthrough in a JAD session 
with the end users, there is a fair chance they'll work when implemented in 
code.).

But not if you start messing with them and "proceduralizing" them.

Basically, you either need an OO approach, or you don't. I haven't yet come 
across a case where a hybrid was indicated, but that possibility certainly 
can't be excluded. If it looked like this was necessary I'd endeavour to 
ensure there was separation via an interface layer and further separation by 
making the OO part a subsystem and the procedural (batch?) another 
subsystem. (Actually, I'd seek vehemently to remove the batch altogether, 
but that's another discussion... :-))



> It really works, and you find that U.S. Government Defense projects
> are almost always specified to use something along these lines these
> days.

Our National Anthem here is called "God Defend New Zealand" (it's pretty 
certain we can't do it ourselves, and since we pissed our allies off it is 
unlikely anyone else would help us...). From what you are saying it looks 
like the US might need to adopt a similar policy... :-)


> For example, the High Level Design Document might be specified to be
> compilable.

I guess these days that's not SO unusual. But compilable to what?

> Languages like Ada really show this mixture very very well. Highly
> Object oriented, both in design and implementation at the top level
…[88 more quoted lines elided]…
> COBOL object.

I see it as a function, not an object.

But (in the ocntext of this discussion) that's kind of splitting hairs, I 
guess.

Thanks for an interesting post, Paul.

I don't entirely agree with all you wrote, but I don't disagree vehemently 
enough to have an argument over it :-)

I did find some of your experience interesting. I've never used ADA although 
a colleague of mine in Germany reckoned it rocks.

You said it handled OO and procedural coding very well. I think you could 
say the same about OO COBOL.

The question is whether you need/want to mix the two. As you may have 
gathered I would strongly oppose that, unless there was some compelling case 
for it.

Usually, where extensive procedural code is "required" is in a perceived 
need for batch processing. I try to exclude this from systems being 
designed, so I don't really need procedural code in a monolithic form. (You 
can validly argue that Object Methods are written in procedural code, but 
this is encapsulated and transparent. It communicates with the outside world 
through an interface layer and interacts with other objects in that layer. 
Such is not the case with monolithic procedural programs.)

Furthermore, I see my objects as components and plug them together and reuse 
them all the time.That means my systems are designed around components and 
Object Classes slot perfectly inot that category.

Nevertheless, if what you have, and the approaches you are using , works for 
you, you won't hear me objecting...:-)

Cheers,

Pete.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Is OO COBOL relevant today or is it unnecessary?

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2010-11-09T09:17:13-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<vhsid6dp76agugpj9qa4s35aqpgl5h7dh1@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<8jrmbeFvo6U1@mid.individual.net> <4cd8d4f1$0$14786$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
I think OO is best for "fitting in", making it easy to be a component
of a larger system.

I'm pretty sure that this isn't the ideal way for everything to go.
Isolation has its advantages, especially in a system optimized for
such isolation.    And personal computers have come up with sandboxes
because of vulnerabilities with non-isolated software.

I am really intrigued with functional languages - partly because we
have to really know what we want, and partially because functions are
so tightly defined - we can isolate them as much as we want.

I foresee some more movement in defining how isolated programs should
be from operating systems and from each other.   I love to be able to
delete or restore an earlier version of a program without risk. That
means, if I restore my Mail app using Apple's Time Machine, I restore
the data (which Apple stores somewhere else - that the average grandma
won't know where it is).

OO is powerful - but with great power comes great responsibility.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Is OO COBOL relevant today or is it unnecessary?

- **From:** Paul <paul-nospamatall.raulerson@mac.com>

```
I am picking parts of this to reply to. We are, in fact, in close 
general agreement, so I am picking a few nits. :) 

   So what all that means to me is that Objects are great for 
   design - but like normalized databases, must usually be    
   "denormalized" (de-objectified?) to actually do the work 
   efficiently.

       Based on a sample of SmallTalk?

       I don't think that's fair... :-)

Oh, I think it is fair, when applied as a general rule at least. The 
closer a language approaches “pure” object orientation, the more 
difficult it is to create ordinary procedural logic with. Conversely, 
the easier it is to create event driven logic. 


    OOP has come a long way since SmallTalk. It isn't that 
    objects need to be de-objectified; it is just a matter of what  
    you want the Class (from which the object is derived) to do.

   And, as we are generally agreeing, there are classes of 
   processing like batch, for which OOP is not ideal. (It can do 
   it,but there are probably better ways...)

OOP has indeed came a long way. I would argue that the “improvements” 
have almost all been in melding procedural languages and methodologies 
with OOP and OOD. 

For example, I suppose most people would call Visual Basic an “Object 
Oriented Language”, though perhaps only partly so. 

Cerrtainly C#, Ada, and Java are object oriented languages, though they 
are usually classed as a “hybrid” language.  

Eiffel, SmallTalk, and Ruby are pretty much examples of “pure” object 
oriented languages. 

 The languages that are most comfortable for procedural languages to 
learn are the hybrid languages, and they are “newer” than Smalltalk. 
And they have also be “deobjectified” at least a bit. That’s why they  
are comfortable- they still contain procedural or imperative 
statements. 


> I think most
> people agree with that point of view, at least people who have done
> large projects (especially real time projects) tend to feel that way.

   Not me. (And I have done some very large projects :-)) If 
   you're going to use OOD then implement it with OOP. There's
   really no need to "de-objectify" your objects.

I suppose it does depend upon the project a bit. If you are doing 
hardware integration, you usually have a lot of low level stuff, like 
device drivers and bus communications.  Directly manipulating a bunch 
of bits, and dealing with hardware engineers.  If the language just 
can’t do it, or won’t allow it, then you are faced with interfaces to 
assembler language, C, or other procedural languages. 

But even for large accounting projects, I find there are lots of areas 
that function better as procedural implementations.

If that is what you are talking about in terms of newer OOP languages, 
then I agree. Java for instance, being a hybrid OO language, allows me 
to get at the bits when I need to, and so does C# and ADA. Pure OO 
languages make that difficult on purpose, and try to make it simply 
impossible. 


(Any more than there is usually a need to "de-normalize" your RDB. I've 
sat in with teams who were proposing to do that. Invariably it was for 
their own convenience, not for increased effficiency. I wouldn't allow 
this on any project unless the DBA OKed it and agreed it would improve 
actual performance.) I'm not saying I would NEVER do it, but I'd want a 
compelling case before permitting it. (So, far, I have seen some 
candidate cases, but tnone that really required it...)

We will just have to disagree on this, as my experience rather directly 
contrasts yours. For example, a normalized contact database may have 20 
or 30 tables. Sure you can use views and logical files and such to make 
that happen, but it will never be as efficient as, for example, a 
totally de-normalized system using a single table/file with variable 
length and “trailer” records.  Less reads. Less writes, less 
processing. More efficient. More difficult to maintain too, but it’s 
all a balancing act. 

It also depends upon what level of normalization you are talking about 
- 1st or 4th?  First level normalized data is often quite usable. 
Fourth level, well - I don’t usually accept it in that form. DBAs do 
squeal until I go make them run explains, after which, they usually are 
quite humble for a few days. 

Oh, and the larger the data sets, the more benefit that can generally 
be derived from a bit of controlled de-normalization, at least to a 
degree. 

     Basically, you either need an OO approach, or you don't. I 
     haven't yet come across a case where a hybrid was 
     indicated, but that possibility certainly can't be excluded.

     If it looked like this was necessary I'd endeavour to ensure 
     there was separation via an interface layer and further 
     separation by making the OO part a subsystem and the 
     procedural (batch?) another subsystem. (Actually, I'd seek 
     vehemently to remove the batch altogether, but that's 
     another discussion... :-))

LOL! Indeed, I know your views on that. :)  But if you use C#, or Java, 
or Ada, or any other hybrid language, you have already devolved from a 
pure OO approach, to a hybrid approach. 

And it is sometimes just a matter of perspective. At a high level, 
something can look like, and indeed, but a very pure object. But at the 
implementation level, once you drill down that deep, it can be purely 
procedural. Indeed, in one sense, it is always procedural, since we 
dont’ have OO Processors yet. Those QBIT computers might come pretty 
close though. 


> It really works, and you find that U.S. Government Defense projects
> are almost always specified to use something along these lines these
> days.

     Our National Anthem here is called "God Defend New 
     Zealand" (it's pretty certain we can't do it ourselves, and 
     since we pissed our allies off it is unlikely anyone else would 
     help us...). From what you are saying it looks like the US 
     might need to adopt a similar policy... :-)

You ever see a P3 or S3 Antisubmarine aircraft zipping by in your sky?  
If so, that is exactly how the software load on those birds works. I 
guarantee that, as I had more than a little something to do with it. :) 


> For example, the High Level Design Document might be specified to be
> compilable.

     I guess these days that's not SO unusual. But compilable to 
     what?

Oh, that’s an easy one. Executable code of course - the same code that 
will be in the final delivery in fact. Kinda gives a new twist to the 
idea of self-documenting code doesn’t it? 

It also makes a lot of sense, because as you drill down more and more 
into the detail, the parts to implement become smaller and smaller - 
and as the coding for them gets more and more procedural, this is a 
very good thing. 

Smaller chunks of code to review, test, and certify. Integration 
testing can be started from day one. And best of all, your programmers 
see their ideas flesh out and take shape, as well as get a feeling of 
accomplishment at each milestone. 

You can do the same kind of thing in OO or Procedural languages of 
course, but the hybrid languages do it really really well.
```

#### ↳ Re: Is OO COBOL relevant today or is it unnecessary?

- **Message-ID:** `<35ea9836-79cf-4a97-b67f-f085466cc411@w38g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<8jrmbeFvo6U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Nov 9, 2:27 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> 1. It is written using Micro Focus language extensions which will be
> unfamiliar to many people.

It seems that the current OO implementations are all incompatible.

> 2. It is just one interpretation of an OO COBOL solution to the problem.
> 3. It was not intended to be a textbook example of "how to write OO COBOL"

> 4. It DOES demonstrate at least one useful facility of OO COBOL (recursion).

Actually MicroFocus COBOL has recursion (for a couple of decades)
without needing OO. It is an implementation issue not a language one.


> However, on this occasion some of the objections don't seem to be about the
> code, but rather the whole concept of Object Oriented Programming in COBOL.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> it.

Exactly, the criteria is 'depending'.

Most applications that were developed in COBOL were based on the
principle of serial reuse. To process a sales order you read through
the order lines one at a time dealing with each one in turn. This
reuses the record area. There is no need to create a 'record object'
for each order line and it is usually incorrect to 'process them all
at once'.

(I recall Robert Wagner once recommended that for process a sequential
file it could be done by creating an object for each record and then
telling them all to read.)

Most applications that lend themselves to OO are not written in COBOL.
Granted that GUIs tend to be OO, or at least pseudo-OO, and one can
write COBOL GUIs.

(I wrote Windows fully graphics COBOL programs for Windows 3.0. Not
just GUI but graphs and 3d views).

As it happens an OO structure can be created using dynamically called
programs. Methods can be emulated by calling with a 'function'
parameter that is used, for example, to dispatch to nested programs

Instead of INVOKE 'dosomething' of Customer ...
it can be CALL Customer USING 'dosomething' ...

The main constraint of this is that there can be many occurrences of
objects while there can be only one module of each name.

The question then is 'do we need more than one of these at a time?'.

> I used OO COBOL to write components for many years. I'd probably still be
> doing it if events hadn't transpired to make me think again about using
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> processing needs to be adoted to move into this camp.

The future is _CURRENTLY_ "Objects and Layers", and will be until the
next new thing comes along.


> The success of languages like Java, C++, C# etc. is NOT because they are
> good for batch processing...

The success of C++ was because it solved many problems that C had, and
which COBOL did not have. It introduced many of its own problems
though. The main one was that C++ allows you, through overloading, to
write your own private language
which may appear alien to another C++ programmer.

Java came along to solve the problems that C++ had created.

C# was created as Microsoft's answer to Java (after they had stopped
being allowed to undermine interoperability in Java).


> Howard posted an excellent response in the thread referenced above, where he
> pointed out that OO COBOL can be used to leverage existing COBOL into the
…[78 more quoted lines elided]…
> using it is not about trying to write C++ in COBOL.
```

a double-take when they saw something like: 12  some-field pic x(abc).

…[9 more quoted lines elided]…

If data is required, it is often useful to have separate instances of it. 
This is well catered for by creating instances of an object.

>
>> However, on this occasion some of the objections don't seem to be
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
> telling them all to read.)

Robert often thought "outside the box". :-)

You are describing a batch use of serial reusability. OO is not good for 
this. On-line serial reusability (where code is re-entered multiple times at 
different specified points (as for CICS and IMS/DC), but never outside those 
points) can benefit from OO because separate instances can be used, rather 
than a single shared resident copy of the code, re-enterable at various 
points. This may or may not have advantages depending on the application.

(From the COBOL POV it can eliminate the need for a GO TO... DEPENDING ON 
which would gladden a number of COBOL people... :-))

>
> Most applications that lend themselves to OO are not written in COBOL.

No they are not. That is because there are better OO languages than COBOL 
available.

> write COBOL GUIs.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> parameter that is used, for example, to dispatch to nested programs

Sure, but why would you, when you can do it simply with OO? Why "emulate a 
method" when you can implement a method?
>
> Instead of INVOKE 'dosomething' of Customer ...
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> objects while there can be only one module of each name.

Precisely. And sometimes that is a major constraint. There is also the fact 
that OO Components have the capability to run anywhere. Your CALL 'Customer" 
won't work from a web page unless it is a CGI or ISAPI routine. An object 
can be created and it's methods invoked without that restriction. 
Furthermore, the object can be invoked remotely, and processed remotely, 
while the CGI/ISAPI is tied to a specific server.
>
> The question then is 'do we need more than one of these at a time?'.

It's a fair question. I suspect that the answer has more to do with 
imagination and possibilities than with technology.
>
>> I used OO COBOL to write components for many years. I'd probably
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> next new thing comes along.

But we can't know there WILL be a replacement and the flexibility obtained 
by using objects and layers seems to indicate it will serve us for a very 
long time to come. Even if there IS a new software breakthrough, it is more 
likely to be compatible with encpasulated objects and layers than it  is 
with static monolithic procedural code.

>
>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> being allowed to undermine interoperability in Java).


Your position on Microsoft is a matter of record, Richard. Duly noted. But 
if you want to have a serious discussion about the topic to hand, no purpose 
is served by making statements that are not matters of fact and cannot be 
substantiated. Your statement is outrageous and libellous.

As an opinion, in an unmoderated Newsgroup, fair enough. But then I have to 
waste time balancing it when I'd rather be discussing the relevance of OO. 
The whole thing is counter-productive.

Instead of just sniping at Microsoft you might like to listen to what the 
person who invented C# says about it.. Given he was largely instrumental in 
developing Borland's Delphi, and left Borland to move to MS, why would you 
think he was interested in Java or replacing Java?

It's like you just can't help taking shots at MS, even when it is irrelevant 
to the topic.

C# had nothing to do with Java. It was created as a fully OO alternative to 
C++ (which is only partially OO; in C# ALL types are objects, in C++ this is 
not the case.)

Certainly, a number of Java programmers have embraced C# (self included) but 
that ISN'T why it was invented.

(Having said that, it is fair to assume that MS are not unhappy about it, 
but that isn't grounds for saying it was the reason they did it...)

The primary reason was to have a fully object typed language, that could 
support the .Net framework. Java and C# both share the common ancestry of 
C++, but neither of them are compatible with it.

On Nov 10, 1:16 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
> > The success of C++ was because it solved many problems that C had, and
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> substantiated. Your statement is outrageous and libellous.

No, it it true. Microsoft bought a licence to Java and the terms of
that licence were that it had to remain interoperable with Sun Java.
Microsoft did not fulfill that requirement. It left essential parts
out. It also added extensions without notifying that they were non-
standard. This made programs written and working on MS Java not work
on any other Sun JVM. Sun sued MS for breach of licence and won. MS
then developed C# as a replacement, as a 'better Java'.

Just use Google (or Bung or some name) and widen your sources of
information beyond Microsoft Marketing Department.
```

…[72 more quoted lines elided]…
> method" when you can implement a method?

Because you could do it 20 years ago with non-OO compilers, and it was
done 20 years ago.

> > Instead of INVOKE 'dosomething' of Customer ...
> > it can be CALL Customer USING 'dosomething' ...
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> while the CGI/ISAPI is tied to a specific server.

I don't know why you think that 'it won't work from a web page'. I
have had COBOL running as CGI for nearly 20 years. Originally on OS/2
with the provided Netscape Server using MS COBOL (MF clone) 4.5. The
same code (with a few lines changed) ran on Windows 3.1 using Xitami
web server and still runs on Linux under Apache.

There are no 'CGI or ISAPI routines' except the interface code that I
wrote myself in COBOL. It only needs 'accept from environment-
variable', 'accept from stdin' and 'display upon stdout'.

Modules are shared between the on-line and the interactive systems,
for example the templating module is the same compiled module. The
same template module will output HTML pages or printed reports
depending on the template provided.

And yes, I do AJAX so the server code can be invoked from the remote
site in much the same way (with a system layer of course).


> > The question then is 'do we need more than one of these at a time?'.
>
> It's a fair question. I suspect that the answer has more to do with
> imagination and possibilities than with technology.

No. It has to do with the needs of the application.


> >> I used OO COBOL to write components for many years. I'd probably
> >> still be doing it if events hadn't transpired to make me think again
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> with static monolithic procedural code.

Procedural code does not have to be 'static and monolithic'.

- **References:** `<8jrmbeFvo6U1@mid.individual.net> <35ea9836-79cf-4a97-b67f-f085466cc411@w38g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <8jsschF9l6U1@mid.individual.net> <9bc97cf4-08b1-4a6d-ac7c-eaaee50b5539@35g2000prb.googlegroups.com> <iafjd6ld2cmpue3puos5rau2sa4rlffqq7@4ax.com>`

```
On Nov 10, 10:33 am, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> On Tue, 9 Nov 2010 10:23:44 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz>
> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> changed as well.

The question was specific to OO COBOL, not COBOL-85 or earlier.  While
there is now a standard for OO COBOL most implementations pre-dated
this and so did it differently in the details.


> Are things different on PCs?   Is there a body of work where code has
> moved from one compiler to another without change?    How common is
> this?

My Web based CGI systems in COBOL would (and will in some cases)
compile and run on OS/2 with Netscape Server, Windows 3.x with
Microsoft's PWS or Xitami Server, Multiuser-DOS with Real-32 Web
Server, Linux with Apache. On Linux it would compile with MicroFocus
or Fujitsu compilers.

There were a few line changes between all these but these were
localised in the 'system' parts. Windows actually needed a different
core module (which called all the others) because of the Windows
message queue.

My SP/2 systems could be compiled with Microfocus or Fujitsu with just
minor differences for Multiuser-DOS or Linux. I have tried a couple of
program under OpenCOBOL and they worked.

The difficult part is the programs that used MicroFocus ADIS (Accept
and Display) for the User Interface. Not many do it as well as MF. It
is difficult to get the exact same feel of the UI.
```

> >>> Instead of INVOKE 'dosomething' of Customer ...
> >>> it can be CALL Customer USING 'dosomething' ...

What you said was:

"Your CALL 'Customer" won't work from a web page unless ..."

> My point is that your COBOL code must be in a recognised CGI/ASAPI directory
> on the web server. (So it can have execute permissions, usually); you can't
> embed the COBOL into an HTML page

Why would you want to embed it in a web page ? I put Javascript in a
web page and it can link back to the server to get data from the
programs if required.

But you said 'CGI' and the CALL 'Customer' WILL work from CGI, or at
least from a COBOL main program that does the cgi interfacing.

And no it does not _have_ to be in the CGI directory. Maybe it does on
Microsoft IIS systems, but they are not the whole world, nor the
majority of web servers.

> (as far as I know... please show an
> example if you think it is possible). You CAN emebed objects into an HTML
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> object?  I don't think so.

Why would you want them embedded in a web page ? Your wording was:
"won't work _from_ a web page" not 'won't work _in_ a web page'.

CGI and ISAPI interfaces work on the server not in the browser.

Perhaps you are confusing server-side and client-side code.

PHP, ASP and similar code may be inside a webpage on the server but
the code is executed by the server when that page is requested. That
mechanism is 'code in page'.  Another way of creating html pages is to
have a program create the html using print (or similar). That is 'page
in code'.

Both of these mix application and presentation. I use templates which
keep these separate. The template can then be html, xml, postscript,
text lines, or whatever without needing to change the code.


> > Procedural code does not have to be 'static and monolithic'.
>
> No, but when it is dynamic and encapsulated it is usually an object... :-)
>

'Usually' means 'most of the time'. As this thread is about how
infrequently OO Cobol is used why do you make the assumption that
Cobol must either be 'static and monolithic' or 'an object' ?

associated with client side Javascript. And there are more round trips
involved with this model.

That is so mindlessly pedantic I am tempted not to respond. I already
answered this question when you first asked it above.

We are discussing Objects (which can be embedded in a web page) vis a vis
CGI code, which can't.

The topic is whether OO COBOL is relevant. If Objects written in it can be
embedded in web pages (and they can), that may be an advantage over a
CGI/ISAPI approach in procedural COBOL, which can't.

Or it mightn't. We won't find out unless it is discussed.

> CGI and ISAPI interfaces work on the server not in the browser.

Oh please...
<snipped stuff pertaining to grandmas and egg sucking>

> Both of these mix application and presentation. I use templates which
> keep these separate. The template can then be html, xml, postscript,
> text lines, or whatever without needing to change the code.
>

A very good approach. Fujitsu provide templates to do exactly this. I have
my own which I wrote before Fujitsu provided theirs.

So now we know how you do it, can we get back to the facts regarding
CGI/ISAPI vs embedded objects?

My point is that there can be some advantages in using objects embedded IN
web pages (as well as Objects running server side in ASP-type code and
activated FROM  a web page, as opposed to procedural COBOL in CGI/ASAPI
activated FROM a web page.)

Obviously, they are both useful approaches but the Objects appeal to me more
because they can be activated both IN and FROM the page

This, to me, seems to be a tick for use of Objects in general, and OO COBOL
in particular, given that these objects can be written in it.


The objects can also run on the Desktop without amendment.

>>> Procedural code does not have to be 'static and monolithic'.
>>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Cobol must either be 'static and monolithic' or 'an object' ?

You demonstrate flawed reasoning.

By your own definition above  my statement can be reconstituted as:

"No, but when it is dynamic and encapsulated it is most of the time an
object... :-)"

or, in more fluent English...

"No, but, most of the times when it is dynamic and encapsulated, it is an
object... :-) "

Your statement as to what the thread is about is completely wrong also (it
has nothing to do with the frequency of use of OO COBOL; it is about whether
it has relevancy for ANY use), but leaving that aside... it is obvious that
the statement is about the occasions when dynamic, encapsulated COBOL is
encountered, and has nothing to do with the size of that domain.

I made no assumption; I made a joke. (Maybe it wasn't a good joke, but at
least it was an attempt.)


Pete.

I still find it very useful for leveraging legacy COBOL into an Object 
world.

Pete.

On Nov 11, 6:29 pm, "Pete Dashwood"

 C$GetEnv does it


> > (Note: some have run time flags that enable stdin and stdout for
> > CONSOLE).
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> either.

Outer Mongolians can still see the results, they just don't have to
download the programs to their own machines.

The current system I am developing is for running a jobbing workshop.
It runs on a box on my desk. The workshop was testing the prototypes
and then started running live while I am still writing code. I'm in
East Coast Bays while the workshop is in Mt Wellington, but could
equally be in Outer Mongolia (except I visited them today).

Touch screens, AJAX interactive, SVG, we don't need no layers or .NET.

> >> So now we know how you do it, can we get back to the facts regarding
> >> CGI/ISAPI vs embedded objects?
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> places where I would have once used OO COBOL.)

Python. I even use Python to extract data from COBOL data files.

I have written OO-COBOL routines that were to be used in a live
project that was to upgrade an existing POS (Point of Sale, not the
other meaning. On second thoughts maybe it was.) COBOL system. I
actually wrote it twice. Once as a '85 CALLed module and again as OO.
Apart from the syntax there was little difference in the structure.
The only advantage of the OO one was that the system could have two or
more instances simultaneously. This was in fact required.

The upgrade was to use Fujitsu for Linux and SP/2 to replace
MicroFocus and make savings on run-times on about 40 sites with 5 - 20
users at each. The MF code was doing raw writes to the printers with
embedded PCL codes.

Fujitsu won't do that. All printing is Postscript. So I had to handle
the print streams with a 'RawPrint' routine/class. It turned out that
one program drove two printers simultaneously (rather that
sequentially) so the OO version would be used.

The project was cancelled by the Australian masters. Instead of
spending 80k to have an upgraded system tailored to their actual needs
(and save 30k per year compared to MF) the Aussie masters had them buy
a system that didn't meet their needs and was several times more
expensive.

The main part of the system would just be standard COBOL. Mixing was
no problem.

> I'm coming to the conclusion after reading the posts here that Charlie is
> probably right and there isn't much point in someone learning OO COBOL now.
>
> I still find it very useful for leveraging legacy COBOL into an Object
> world.
```

On Nov 11, 9:26 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> > Fujitsu won't do that. All printing is Postscript.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

According to the User Guide:

"""Printing Device

In print processing the PostScript Level-1 file is outputted. In this
case, the forms can be printed directly on a PostScript printer."""

This is not a problem on Unix/Linux/Mac where CUPS is used because it
will convert PostScript to whatever the actual printer requires.

But you can't put arbitrary control characters in the stream, they
bork the printing.
```

There could be IF and a bloody big IF, somebody could come up with a 
reasonable price. If there were some mysterious group of COBOLERs who 
truly wanted the language to stay relevant, and that means Procedural, 
and for OO (1) Classes/Methods (2) A rich set of support classes, which 
where necessary also included 'Systems classes', (e.g. one-liner methods 
that take you into APIs where the general classes didn't do it) and well 
documented (samples) for GUI and Internet usage - then it could well 
stand a chance. Sadly it isn't going to happen with a volunteer team, no 
matter how proficient they are - I tried and got no response. Guess I 
must have pissed him off.

I would happily go for such a model as I'm describing, and wouldn't even 
mind contributing where I could. But, overall don't expect 'volunteers' 
to do it as a freebie when IBM, F/J and M/F pay their excellent people 
big bucks. I would envisage say something like an initial $500 from the 
developer and say an annual Maintenance fee of $100. Having written 
that, those two figures are WRITTEN IN STONE, with the proviso that 
there is a fair increase in the two figures based on the Cost of Living 
Index.

Such an approach could only come from an independent body. There is no 
way competitive COBOL vendors could have compromised/agreed on a J4 
approach to the whole problem. Well maybe that's not true - perhaps it 
could be achieved miraculously by 2114.

Think in terms of Java and C# and the dotNet game. No ANSI or ISO 
imprimaturs - solely owned, now by Oracle and Microsoft. When you (Pete) 
and I first got into softwaresimple we rather tended to enter the game 
naively. Recall I suggested perhaps the Collection classes, with no 
knowledge of the F/J format, (and this is now over ten years ago), could 
be decided by J4 and both F/J and M/F could adapt their classes with 
methods which in turn accessed the J4 methods - what a joke ! It 
wouldn't have been trying to meld apples with oranges, but more like 
melding a 747 with a yellow school bus !

Jimmy

On 11/11/2010 2:24 PM, Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Thu, 11 Nov 2010 13:09:02 -0700, James Gavan<jgavan@shaw.ca>
> wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> compared to working with some other languages or even writing our own.

Well I for one would have the satisfaction of working on something I 
really enjoy. But I understand your point.

Knowing that you want to get into games in your retirement when that 
occurs, but for the PRICE, the existing Net Express (as lowly as my 
Version 3.1 is), would I'd wager do your games. There is a Drawing 
Surface class for shapes, which includes basic methods for - 3DBox, Arc, 
Chord, Circle, Ellipse..... and the list goes on - Basically you use 
those methodnames with combinations of x, y, w and h. Then of course you 
use Class Pen to colourise. (How do you like the last word - an 
Americanism spelled out the 'English' way :-).

The old M/F DOS has a Tic Tac Doe ( Noughts and crosses to us Limeys), 
which would be a doddle to revamp with GUI classes etc. Not much fun 
though. As you know, place your symbol in the right boxes and the clever 
computer can't win - but you can get a stalemate.

Me, I'd like to see an inter-active version of Nine Man's Morris (I'm 
guessing that's an allusion to the Cornish Men dancing with straw hats 
and handkerchiefs tied around their knee-stocking tops - probably goes 
back to the Middle Ages. They look a little like an anglicized version 
of boozy Bavarians in lederhosen.). I've got a box, which I haven't 
dragged out for years, I think you call it Bacchus. Just checked :-

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nine_Men's_Morris	

and there is already an on-line version.

> And it doesn't look like a profitable project either.

And as much as I'd like it to, I don't think it will fly. Sad part is, 
and is it part of our current 'so modern' culture, the Great Throw-away 
Society. ? People don't give a toss when they perceive something is 
beyond its shelf life ? Even those with decades in COBOL don't give a 
toss about its continued existence. Don't confine that remark to COBOL. 
I was browsing the Web - two examples

(1) Joe Blow used to code in Smalltalk; he followed where the money was 
- switched to Java. He commented he enjoyed and preferred to work in 
Smalltalk and cold do things in SmallTalk in a much more efficient 
manner than in Java. My guess - he could make that statement because he 
had been using Smalltalk perhaps for a couple of decades, knew the 
language inside out, and spotted the shortcomings in Java. The only 
reference book I have, sanctioned by Sun Micro, makes the point that 
ex-Calgarian James Gosling and team had to take two shots at Collection 
classes before they got it right.

(2) Bill Bloggins codes in C#. Used to code in Java. "So what ?", was 
his response. People just move on. The language du jour is C#.

As for the future, somebody recently, it may have been Pete or Richard, 
referred to the future as regards programming. (We *know* it wasn't John 
Cullleton - his mindset is still in COBOL 85 :-) ).

Anyway many moons ago, I expressed a thought in that OO COBOL group we 
had, SoftwareSimple, way ten years back. You'll probably remember this - 
supermarkets, back before the freezer section kicked in you could buy 
packages of freeze-dried veggies like peas. They just sat on the same 
shelves as lentils etc., no coolers required. You put them into water 
and boiled them until ready.

Speculatively I referred to the freeze-dried peas in the OO group, 
asking/suggesting that when we could switch off a computer, and tomorrow 
start it up so that it began where we left off. Example - you could have 
been two-thirds of the way through entering a Customer's details in a 
Customer Edit Dialog Box.

"Oh" replied Anne Bennett/Wallace the IBM rep on J4. (Watch Bill K. 
chide me if I incorrectly added the 'e' to her name :-) ). She then gave 
it the official or technical name. "You are talking about Persistent 
Objects". Pete's ready to attend COBOL's funeral in 2014; me I'll stick 
my neck out and predict that Persistent Objects will be the next MAJOR 
leap in programming.

You won't be able to tell me I'm wrong. I wont be around. I'm an 
octogenarian next September.

Jimmy, Calgary, AB
```

Ways of persisting objects (and even better ways of persisting objects) have 
been under discussion for at least 15 years. (Unlikely to be the "next big 
thing"). There are now a number of mechanisms for doing it. OO databases are 
one way. Serializing and storing on a standard RDB are another which seems 
to work pretty well. (I use the facilities in C# to serialize and 
deserialize objects I need to have persisted and it all works fine...)

north as Inverness.

James Gavan wrote:

I think the point that has been missed by some of the vendors is that COBOL 
is no longer "the only game in town".

For decades it was, and people really had no choice but to implement the 
procedural code.

Now there are options. And some of those options are excellent. Better than 
using COBOL...

I can't see anyone opting to pay runtime fees for whatever they develop, 
when they don't HAVE to. I mentioned it elsewhere. If you are not a hobbyist 
then the code you write sooner or later has to pay for stuff. If it is 
uneconomic to write (and runtime fees render it so) then you will look for 
another option. Personally, I simply can't afford to buy an expensive laptop 
and not have it pay for itself. Free compilers and free IDE (especially when 
they are outstanding) are a big help towards achieving that ROI.

Big businesses with substantial IT budgets may well swallow it if it means 
they don't have to retrain. SMEs and independents simply can't afford it. It 
is far cheaper to bite the bullet and learn a new (free) language, 
especially if it is possible to leverage your existing investment in COBOL 
into something else, without it costing an arm and a leg.

COBOL. They have a vested interest in keeping COBOL relevant. Nevertheless, 
they know that the little people (SMEs, independents) are not going to be

(Banks, Insurance, etc.) who are targeted by it. And once they are moved to 
.Net, they will find that the .Net languages actually allow them to do 
things better than OO COBOL. It will take a few years and the people who are 
migrating to .NET COBOL will realise that, although it is a cheaper 
platform, it isn't a BETTER one, UNLESS you use it as intended (Objects and 
Layers). At that point they start looking at .Net languages and after that

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Is OO COBOL relevant today or is it unnecessary?

_(thread depth: 13)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-11-16T11:26:02+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8kdqbsF7nqU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<8jsschF9l6U1@mid.individual.net> <9bc97cf4-08b1-4a6d-ac7c-eaaee50b5539@35g2000prb.googlegroups.com> <8ju81vFnvvU1@mid.individual.net> <f52f901e-1633-4ed0-bab6-76c6ea655b9a@v20g2000prl.googlegroups.com> <8k11qbFpjcU1@mid.individual.net> <c5fbb76f-a6cb-4af4-b351-aa3e258c4909@n32g2000pre.googlegroups.com> <8k1d9nFfqnU1@mid.individual.net> <e99d3687-979a-4f27-896c-e879b8f26205@i32g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <A3YCo.13435$xb.6309@newsfe10.iad> <8k3a9sF6kdU1@mid.individual.net> <fp83e6hgvm035ji5pbop8kgs6dr7lhbedu@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Fri, 12 Nov 2010 11:50:34 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> seen, changing mindsets can be difficult.

It CAN happen but it usually takes a considerable time. My own mind was 
changed about COBOL. But it wasn't until I had hands on experience and 
developed an understanding of other approaches that I was able to see it 
dispassionately.

Many people don't have the time or the inclination to get into things that 
are new to them. They expect what they know to be adapted so it can 
encompass newer technology, but then they grizzle about it not looking like 
what they know any more. :-)

After years of using a certain tool in a certain way, it is easy to believe 
that there is no need for newer tools.

I am still amazed by the increase in my personal productivity when I'm NOT 
using COBOL. Yesterday I designed, wrote, tested, and debugged a web service 
in around 3 hours. I then wrote a COM component in C# that can interact 
seamlessly with COBOL (It looks just like a COBOL COM component but it can 
do things that COBOL can't do easily (like invoke the above mentioned web 
service - 2 lines of code), access the system registry without using API 
calls, and kick off multiple threads with asynchronous delegation.) By the 
end of the day (around 9 hours) I had a fully functioning, debugged 
application that can be called by COBOL and does some really cool stuff. It 
will take me the next 4 days just to document it properly :-)

The point is that there is much more satisfaction and real joy in developing 
stuff when you can see it take shape very quickly.

I don't know how long it would have taken me to do the job in COBOL. By the 
time I found out what APIs to use and buggered about with getting the right 
formats for their parameters, with no sample code, and the trial and error 
required for trail blazing, I reckon I could have spent a day just trying to 
get information on how to do what I needed to. I would conservatively 
estimate the overall effort as being at least 3 days in COBOL. (I'm not sure 
how I would write a web service in COBOL and I don't have any tools that 
could generate all the WSDL for me like VS 2008 does, so you can take a day 
just writing SOAP...) This service uses a structure which contains numbers, 
dates, and strings, most of which can be null, so the SOAP is non-trivial. I 
inspected thestuff that VS wrote in about 10 milliseconds (generated from 
the definitions in my program code) and couldn't fault it. Sure enough, when 
I tried it on my local web server it all worked perfectly.

It isn't about new being better than old. It is about things working as you 
intend them to, right off the bat, with the consequent decrease in stress 
levels. Instead of getting angry and frustrated because some API needs 
something passed by content and you spent several hours trying to get it to 
work by reference, because there is no available COBOL documentation for it 
and the docs that ARE there are in VB or C++, I find myself actually 
grinning as I step through code and it just works. This means I can give a 
lot more thought to functionality and produce better applications.

For me, at least, there can be no going back... :-)

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Is OO COBOL relevant today or is it unnecessary?

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2010-11-09T10:35:03-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<aOCdnRBY3cGE90TRnZ2dnUVZ_vudnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<8jrmbeFvo6U1@mid.individual.net> <35ea9836-79cf-4a97-b67f-f085466cc411@w38g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`

```
When people say OO programming is not good for "x", I think it mostly means 
they just don't like OO programming for whatever reason. What makes OO COBOL 
not as good for batch programming as regular COBOL?  Isn't regular COBOL a 
subset of OO COBOL?  Just because the OO features are there does not mean 
that you have to use them, or at least you can minimize their use when the 
situation calls for it..

Like Pete I never really got OO programming until I learned Java.  I still 
did not grasp why it was so popular until I read a book on design patterns. 
The design patterns book showed me clever ways of combining objects (think 
functionality) to solve a wide range of problems.  Prior to this I primarily 
thought of  each PERFORMed paragraph or Called module as performing a single 
function.  I think structured programming influenced my thinking on this. 
I have to admit that most of the business programs that I wrote did not need 
the extra power of the design patterns. Almost all of the business programs 
that I have written have involved serially invoking one modular 
function/procedure/module after another, and this worked very well.

Objects can be more versatile (some might say more complicated) than serial 
use of functionality.  Objects can have many methods and objects can be 
combined using composition or inheritance.  This involves combining 
functionality in ways that I had not previously considered, yielding 
increased power of expression.  You can get some of the OO benefits via 
manual coding in that style but an OO programming language makes it much 
easier.  In the past most COBOL applications did not need any of these extra 
capabilities, but as applications have moved to the internet,  to gui 
interfaces, to components etc. these extra capabilities start to come in 
handy.

So is OO the end all and be all?  Of course not.  If you look at the Java 
library to see how some of it is implemented, at the bottommost layer you 
will still see native routines written in C++.  Is OO design and OO 
programming the answer to everthing?  No, and it can be hard to get it 
right.  The Java I/O features are a good example of this, as the supposedly 
highly skilled programmers at Sun have redone I/O more than once.  Without a 
knowledge of the design patterns some of I/O implementation does not seem to 
make sense when you first see it.

Back to the question, is OO COBOL relevant? Yes it might be, if you want to 
be able to use some of your existing COBOL in the network environment.  But 
the cost of the compilers is high and the learning curve is steep.  One 
criticism of OO is the new terminology and OO COBOL only adds to this with 
terms like "Repository" and "Factory" which other OO languages do not use. 
If you are like me and Pete you might have to learn Java or C++ or C# first 
before you can grasp OO COBOL.  If  you do that then OO COBOL will seem much 
more verbose compared to those languages.    Java and C# 
compilers/interpreters are free but after you lean one of them you still 
face the problem of what to do with your legacy COBOL programs.  At this 
point in time if you can possibly skip learning and using OO COBOL then I 
would recommend doing just that.  Find a tool that you like that can convert 
your COBOL into OO language "y" and learn that language rather than OO 
COBOL.


"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:35ea9836-79cf-4a97-b67f-f085466cc411@w38g2000pri.googlegroups.com...
On Nov 9, 2:27 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> 1. It is written using Micro Focus language extensions which will be
> unfamiliar to many people.

It seems that the current OO implementations are all incompatible.

> 2. It is just one interpretation of an OO COBOL solution to the problem.
> 3. It was not intended to be a textbook example of "how to write OO COBOL"

> 4. It DOES demonstrate at least one useful facility of OO COBOL 
> (recursion).

Actually MicroFocus COBOL has recursion (for a couple of decades)
without needing OO. It is an implementation issue not a language one.


> However, on this occasion some of the objections don't seem to be about 
> the
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> it.

Exactly, the criteria is 'depending'.

Most applications that were developed in COBOL were based on the
principle of serial reuse. To process a sales order you read through
the order lines one at a time dealing with each one in turn. This
reuses the record area. There is no need to create a 'record object'
for each order line and it is usually incorrect to 'process them all
at once'.

(I recall Robert Wagner once recommended that for process a sequential
file it could be done by creating an object for each record and then
telling them all to read.)

Most applications that lend themselves to OO are not written in COBOL.
Granted that GUIs tend to be OO, or at least pseudo-OO, and one can
write COBOL GUIs.

(I wrote Windows fully graphics COBOL programs for Windows 3.0. Not
just GUI but graphs and 3d views).

As it happens an OO structure can be created using dynamically called
programs. Methods can be emulated by calling with a 'function'
parameter that is used, for example, to dispatch to nested programs

Instead of INVOKE 'dosomething' of Customer ...
it can be CALL Customer USING 'dosomething' ...

The main constraint of this is that there can be many occurrences of
objects while there can be only one module of each name.

The question then is 'do we need more than one of these at a time?'.

> I used OO COBOL to write components for many years. I'd probably still be
> doing it if events hadn't transpired to make me think again about using
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> processing needs to be adoted to move into this camp.

The future is _CURRENTLY_ "Objects and Layers", and will be until the
next new thing comes along.


> The success of languages like Java, C++, C# etc. is NOT because they are
> good for batch processing...

The success of C++ was because it solved many problems that C had, and
which COBOL did not have. It introduced many of its own problems
though. The main one was that C++ allows you, through overloading, to
write your own private language
which may appear alien to another C++ programmer.

Java came along to solve the problems that C++ had created.

C# was created as Microsoft's answer to Java (after they had stopped
being allowed to undermine interoperability in Java).


> Howard posted an excellent response in the thread referenced above, where 
> he
…[87 more quoted lines elided]…
> using it is not about trying to write C++ in COBOL.
```

In what way am I being patronizing?  One of the things I like about COBOL is 
that I find it easier to read than languages like C, C++,  C# and Java. 
However I find the OO COBOL syntax a big step in the wrong direction as far 
as readability is concerned. The source code overhead for each method is too 
great.  I do understand why it is done that way and it was quite an 
achievement to retrofit OO onto COBOL.  There are lots of little ways that 
COBOL is more concise than than OO languages like Java, but even adding up 
all those does not make up for the OO COBOL method overhead.  This is just 
my opinion and as we all know opinion do vary. I don't see how that makes me 
condescending.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ OT: Grammar

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2010-11-10T12:20:11-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:c848b4f3-306d-4c2d-b29d-a411ce96a737@n32g2000prc.googlegroups.com...

> In what way am I being patronizing?

By using second person active voice excessively.

"you might have to learn Java or C++ or C# first before you can grasp
OO COBOL."
"If  you do that then .."
"but after you lean ..."
"you still face the problem .."
"if you can possibly skip learning .."
"Find a tool that you like .. and learn that .."

<a href="http://www.google.co.nz/url?
sa=t&source=web&cd=2&sqi=2&ved=0CBkQFjAB&url=http%3A%2F
%2Fwww.englishsoftware.org%2Farticles%2Fimprove-english-writing

How To Write In The Second Person Without Patronizing Your Readers ...
</a>

<snip>

Thanks Richard. I was not aware that overusing "you" could be taken as 
patronizing.  My use of it was not a conscious choice to be patronizing. 
When I looked up "How To Write In The Second Person Without Patronizing Your 
Readers" on google I did not see any suggestions on what to use instead. 
What do you suggest?  Using "one" sounds pretentious to my ear and using 
"we" will get me in trouble with docdwarf as he is the only roalty posting 
here.  Is this a cultural difference or a difference between American 
English and British style English? Or perhaps it isjust something I have 
failed to learn.

- **In-Reply-To:** `<QaKdnVmBS_qnSUfRnZ2dnUVZ_vCdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<8jrmbeFvo6U1@mid.individual.net> <35ea9836-79cf-4a97-b67f-f085466cc411@w38g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <aOCdnRBY3cGE90TRnZ2dnUVZ_vudnZ2d@earthlink.com> <9fa547b6-509c-46b7-bc13-36fe06f87bf6@n24g2000prj.googlegroups.com> <MdCdnXnkU7S3TETRnZ2dnUVZ_gednZ2d@earthlink.com> <c848b4f3-306d-4c2d-b29d-a411ce96a737@n32g2000prc.googlegroups.com> <QaKdnVmBS_qnSUfRnZ2dnUVZ_vCdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
On 11/10/2010 10:20 AM, Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Richard"<riplin@Azonic.co.nz>  wrote in message
> news:c848b4f3-306d-4c2d-b29d-a411ce96a737@n32g2000prc.googlegroups.com...
…[46 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Like Pete, I don't know why Richard would take exception to "you", and 
bearing in mind both he, (Richard), and I are ex-pats from the UK.

You picked up on the royal "we". I can't be certain, but I don't think 
even Queen Elizabeth uses that now. I think the Doc is the only one that 
makes claim to that term :-).

There's the story that goes, Elizabeth and Prince Philip were presented 
before King George VI at some event. Philip was wearing a kilt. He 
curtsied to the King. The apocryphal answer is that Georgie exclaimed, 
"We are not amused !".

I'd guess back in the thirties when QEII was being educated, from her 
upper-crust training she may well have used the "one" idiom - 
conceivably she has dropped that too. She pre-records an annual 
Christmas Message, broadcast by BBC TV and copies of the tape finish up 
in certainly the 'Anglo' parts of the former British 
Empire/Commonwealth. From her earliest inception on TV, not that I ever 
watch it, her accent is less 'upper harsh' and there appears to be a 
genuine warmth in the lady.

 From my experience in the UK until I was 45, the only ones I recall 
using 'one' were the artsy-fartsy crowd or 'delicate' actors. "One 
doesn't do it this way, does one ?". It is bloody pretentious in modern 
English usage for sure in UK and would be laughed at here in Canada.

Sometimes it's tricky, whether spoken or written, to convey what you 
really intend. As much as possible I try to qualify my meaning, but it 
doesn't always work. I try to avoid "You write this routine or method in 
this way", which implies my way is the only solution; so, I try and 
remember to change that to, "You can/could write this routine or method 
in this way".

BTW liked your bit about COBOL usage.

Jimmy
```

Richard wrote:
> On Nov 10, 11:54 am, "Charles Hottel" <chot...@earthlink.net> wrote:
>> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>>

>>
>> In what way am I being patronizing?
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> </a>

I managed to get this link working and read the article.

It is pretty much another statement of what I already posted elsewhere about 
the use of "you" where the impersonal "one" used to serve.

If one looks at the examples of "patronization" posted above, it becomes 
obvious that this is a very personal and subjective thing.

For myself, I could see nothing patronizing or condescending in either the 
examples or in what Charlie wrote so I was interested to try and understand 
how someone else could.

The conclusion I have reached is that if someone is looking for offence they 
will usually find it.

If the basic premise that modern conversational English is recognised in 
writing as it is in speech, is accepted, there should be no reason for 
offence to be taken.

But Hey! some people are offended if you say: "Hello!"

(This was covered in a  sketch from Peter Cook and Dudley Moore (it was 
banned for a while on the basis of the language it contained, but it was 
still very funny...) many years ago, called "Derek and Clive"...)

"This bloke come up to me and says 'Hello!'

"Provocative fucker...! What'd you do?"

"I kicked him in the nuts..."


>
>> One of the things I like about COBOL is
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>> condescending.

It doesn't.

Don't worry about it.

Pete.

```
Richard wrote:
> On Nov 10, 4:35 am, "Charles Hottel" <chot...@earthlink.net> wrote:
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> applications that have been developed over decades.

There definitely is a problem with differences between OO COBOL Compilers. 
No argument.


> You can't just
> rewrite one called module as OO and expect it to fit into the
> application.

I think you can. But it does depend on the module and the application.

If you have a series of existing COBOL called modules and they all work 
fine, then it is definitely possible to wrap them as OO and they will still 
work fine. The reason you might do this is because you may be extending the 
existing application with added features that were written in an OO laguage 
and you want to interact between the old and the new.

At least in the Fujitsu environment (I'm not sure about other OO COBOL 
environments) it is possible for non-OO COBOL code to invoke OO COBOL 
objects. This is useful, but it is better (for interoperability) if all of 
the components are objects. I use the Component Object Model (COM) so I like 
it if all of the objects comply with this as it makes layering and 
interfacing much simpler. The source language is no longer important and 
everything just behaves as pieces of functionality.

>
>
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>> components etc. these extra capabilities start to come in handy.

I agree.

>>
>> So is OO the end all and be all? Of course not. If you look at the
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> You are being patronizing.

Huh?!

I can't find anything "patronizing" in the above. A man expresses his 
opinion and suggests some actions.

(possibly I'm biased because I agree with what he wrote, but I certainly 
can't see anything patronizing in the tone of the writing.)

Sometimes there is sensitivity around the word "you" but it is pretty 
obvious that Charlie has used it instead of writing "one". It is NOT meant 
to be a personal command directed at the reader.

I often have the same problem. I may be writing impersonally but using "one" 
everywhere is uncomfortable and unnatural to me. (It sounds like some 
pompous Upper Class English twit). It is necessary to rely on the reader to 
recognize that "you" is not being used personally, it is just common 
colloquial usage as people speak in conversation every day.

I didn't feel patronized reading the above. I guess YMMV.

>
> Actually I have written OO Cobol but most of my work for the last few
> years has been with OO Python. I find Java to be clumsy and verbose.

I find that interesting.

From the brief acquaintance I made with Python (based on Richard's 
unpatronizing recommendation :-)) it certainly seems like a very useful 
language.

Might be worth another thread with some examples...

Pete.
